563<div id="idm139818004390304" lang="en" class="tsec sec">

564<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="idm139818004390304title">

565Abstract</h2>

566<!--article-meta-->

567<div>

568<p id="P1" class="p p-first-last">

569Cannabinoids are a group of compounds that mediate their effects through cannabinoid receptors. The discovery of &#x00394;<sup>

5709</sup>

571-tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) as the major psychoactive principle in marijuana, as well as the identification of cannabinoid receptors and their endogenous ligands, has led to a significant growth in research aimed at understanding the physiological functions of cannabinoids. Cannabinoid receptors include CB1, which is predominantly expressed in the brain, and CB2, which is primarily found on the cells of the immune system. The fact that both CB1 and CB2 receptors have been found on immune cells suggests that cannabinoids play an important role in the regulation of the immune system. Recent studies demonstrated that administration of THC into mice triggered marked apoptosis in T cells and dendritic cells, resulting in immunosuppression. In addition, several studies showed that cannabinoids downregulate cytokine and chemokine production and, in some models, upregulate T-regulatory cells (Tregs) as a mechanism to suppress inflammatory responses. The endocannabinoid system is also involved in immunoregulation. For example, administration of endocannabinoids or use of inhibitors of enzymes that break down the endocannabinoids, led to immunosuppression and recovery from immune-mediated injury to organs such as the liver. Manipulation of endocannabinoids and/or use of exogenous cannabinoids <em>

572in vivo</em>

573 can constitute a potent treatment modality against inflammatory disorders. This review will focus on the potential use of cannabinoids as a new class of anti-inflammatory agents against a number of inflammatory and autoimmune diseases that are primarily triggered by activated T cells or other cellular immune components.</p>

574</div>

575</div>

576<div id="idm139818008088368" class="tsec sec">

577<h2 class="headless nomenu">

578</h2>

579<p id="P2" class="p p-first">

580Cannabis, commonly known as marijuana, is a product of the <em>

581Cannabis sativa</em>

582 plant and the active compounds from this plant are collectively referred to as <strong>

583cannabinoids</strong>

584. For several centuries, marijuana has been used as an alternative medicine in many cultures and, recently, its beneficial effects have been shown in: the treatment of nausea and vomiting associated with cancer chemotherapy; anorexia and cachexia seen in HIV/AIDS patients; and in neuropathic pain and spasticity in multiple sclerosis [<a href="#R1" rid="R1" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461365">

5851</a>

586&#x02013;<a href="#R4" rid="R4" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461461">

5874</a>

588]. Cannabinoid pharmacology has made important advances in recent years after the discovery of the <strong>

589cannabinoid receptors</strong>

590 (CB1 and CB2). Cannabinoid receptors and their endogenous ligands have provided an excellent platform for the investigation of the therapeutic effects of cannabinoids. It is well known that CB1 and CB2 are heterotrimeric G<sub>

591i/o</sub>

592-protein-coupled receptors and that they are both expressed in the periphery and the CNS. However, CB1 expression is predominant in the CNS, especially on presynaptic nerves, and CB2 is primarily expressed on immune cells [<a href="#R5" rid="R5" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461508">

5935</a>

594,<a href="#R6" rid="R6" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461444">

5956</a>

596].</p>

597<p id="P3">

598Arachidonic acid metabolites have been shown to exhibit properties similar to compounds found in <em>

599Cannabis sativa</em>

600. These metabolites are hence referred to as endocannabinoids. These ubiquitous endogenous cannabinoids act as natural ligands for the cannabinoid receptors expressed in mammalian tissue, thus constituting an important lipid-signaling system termed the endocannabinoid system. The endocannabinoid system is an important biological regulatory system that has been shown to be highly conserved from lower invertebrates to higher mammals [<a href="#R7" rid="R7" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461436">

6017</a>

602]. Other than the lipid transmitters that serve as ligands for the cannabinoid receptors, the endocannabinoid family also comprises the enzymes for biosynthesis and degradation of the ligands. The endocannabinoids include <em>

603N</em>

604-arachidonoylethanolamine, anandamide (AEA), 2-arachidonoyl glycerol (2-AG), <em>

605N</em>

606-arachydonoyldopamine, noladin ether and virodhamine. AEA was discovered by Devane <em>

607et al</em>

608. and is an amide formed from arachidonic acid and ethanolamine [<a href="#R8" rid="R8" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461388">

6098</a>

610]. AEA binds to brain CB1 with high affinity and mimics the behavioral actions of exogenous cannabinoid &#x00394;<sup>

6119</sup>

612-tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) when injected into rodents. 2-AG was discovered independently 3 years later by Mechoulam <em>

613et al</em>

614. [<a href="#R9" rid="R9" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461448">

6159</a>

616] and Sugiura <em>

617et al</em>

618. [<a href="#R10" rid="R10" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461494">

61910</a>

620]. It was found to exist in much higher concentration in serum and brain than AEA. 2-AG has similar affinities for both CB1 and CB2 receptors, as does AEA, but it exhibits higher efficacy. Endocannabinoids are derivatives of arachidonic acid conjugated with either ethanolamine or glycerol. They are synthesized on demand from phospholipid precursors residing in the cell membrane in response to a rise in intracellular calcium levels. Inside cells, endocannabinoids are catalytically hydrolyzed by the aminohydrolase fatty acid amide hydrolase (FAAH), which degrades AEA into arachidonic acid and ethanolamine [<a href="#R11" rid="R11" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461429">

62111</a>

622]. 2-AG is hydrolyzed into AEA and glycerol by either FAAH or by monoacyl glycerol lipase (MAGL). Fatty acid-binding proteins (FABPs) have been reported to play an important role as intracellular carriers in the transport of AEA from the plasma membrane to FAAH for their subsequent inactivation [<a href="#R12" rid="R12" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461366">

62312</a>

624]. Studies to date indicate that the main pharmacological function of the endocannabinoid system is in neuromodulation: controlling motor functions, cognition, emotional responses, homeostasis and motivation. However, in the periphery, this system is an important modulator of the ANS, immune system and microcirculation [<a href="#R13" rid="R13" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_327953579">

62513</a>

626]. Some well-known natural and synthetic cannabinoids and endocannabinoids are depicted in <a href="/pmc/articles/PMC2828614/table/T1/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="T1" rid-ob="ob-T1" co-legend-rid="">

627<span>

628Table 1</span>

629</a>

630.</p>

631<!--table ft1-->

632<!--table-wrap mode="anchored" t5-->

633<div class="table-wrap anchored whole_rhythm" id="T1">

721<td align="left" valign="top" rowspan="1" colspan="1">

722<div data-largeobj="" data-largeobj-link-rid="largeobj_idm139818000296272" class="figure">

725<div id="largeobj_idm139818000296272" class="largeobj-link align_right" style="display: none">

733</div>

734<div id="largeobj_idm139818001510048" class="largeobj-link align_right" style="display: none">

735<a target="object" rel="noopener" href="/pmc/articles/PMC2828614/table/T1/?report=objectonly">

736Open in a separate window</a>

737</div>

738</div>

739<p id="P4" class="p">

740Cannabinoids are potent anti-inflammatory agents and they exert their effects through induction of apoptosis, inhibition of cell proliferation, suppression of <strong>

741cytokine</strong>

742 production and induction of T-regulatory cells (Tregs). In this review, we provide an in-depth description of all four different mechanisms and we further discuss the immunosuppressive properties of cannabinoids in the context of inflammatory and <strong>

743autoimmune disease</strong>

744 states, triggered by cellular rather than humoral components of the immune system.</p>

745</div>

746<div id="S1" class="tsec sec">

747<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S1title">

748Apoptotic effects of cannabinoids on immune cell populations</h2>

749<p id="P5" class="p p-first">

750One major mechanism of immunosupression by cannabinoids is the induction of cell death or apoptosis in immune cell populations. Under normal conditions, apoptosis is required in order to maintain homeostasis and it involves morphological changes (i.e., cell shrinkage, nuclear fragmentation and membrane blebbing) as well as molecular changes (i.e., induction of caspases and cytochrome c leakage) [<a href="#R14" rid="R14" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461373">

75114</a>

752]. The extrinsic pathway of apoptosis is initiated with the ligation of death receptors (i.e., CD95) on the cell surface, leading to activation of major caspases, such as caspase 3, 8 and 10. The intrinsic pathway of apoptosis is initiated via mitochondria and caspase 9; cytochrome c and caspase 3 are the major players in the induction of cell death [<a href="#R14" rid="R14" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461395">

75314</a>

754,<a href="#R15" rid="R15" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461432">

75515</a>

756].</p>

757<p id="P6">

758&#x00394;<sup>

7599</sup>

760-THC and its apoptotic effects on immune cell populations have been studied extensively: in 1998, Zhu <em>

761et al.</em>

762 demonstrated that <em>

763in vitro</em>

764 THC induced apoptosis in murine macrophages and T cells. This study also showed that the process was mediated via activation of Bcl-2 and caspases [<a href="#R16" rid="R16" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461516">

76516</a>

766]. It was difficult to demonstrate the apoptotic effects of THC on lymphocytes, <em>

767in vivo</em>

768, and our laboratory speculated that this might be due to rapid clearance of dead cells by phagocytic cells. Therefore, we exposed C57BL/6 mice to 10 mg/kg bodyweight THC and, after several time points, (4, 6, 24 and 72 h), obtained lymphocytes from the thymus and spleen of these animals. The cells were incubated for 12&#x02013;24 h <em>

769ex vivo</em>

770 and, since the phagocytosis was excluded in the cultures, we detected significant levels of THC-induced apoptosis in T cells, B cells and macrophages [<a href="#R17" rid="R17" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461374">

77117</a>

772]. We have also demonstrated that THC induced higher levels of apoptosis in naive lymphocytes, when compared with mitogen-activated lymphocytes, because activated cells downregulated the levels of CB2 on their cell surface [<a href="#R17" rid="R17" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461353">

77317</a>

774]. Several studies also reported THC-induced apoptosis in antigen-presenting cells. In bone marrow-derived dendritic cells (DCs), THC induced apoptosis via ligation of both CB1 and CB2 and activation of caspases such as caspase 2, 8 and 9. <em>

775In vivo</em>

776, THC administration decreased the number of splenic DCs, as well as MHCII expression by DCs [<a href="#R18" rid="R18" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461450">

77718</a>

778,<a href="#R19" rid="R19" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461364">

77919</a>

780]. Furthermore, THC increased Bcl-2 and caspase 1 activity in naive and lipopolysaccharide (LPS)-activated macrophages isolated from the peritoneal cavity of mice [<a href="#R16" rid="R16" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461477">

78116</a>

782].</p>

783<p id="P7">

784Other natural and synthetic cannabinoid compounds (CBD, AEA, ajulemic acid [AjA] and JWH-015), whose structures are depicted in <a href="/pmc/articles/PMC2828614/table/T1/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="T1" rid-ob="ob-T1" co-legend-rid="">

785<span>

786Table 1</span>

787</a>

788, have also been shown to induce apoptosis in murine and human T lymphocytes. Cannabidiol, the nonpsychoactive ingredient in cannabis, induced apoptosis in CD4<sup>

789+</sup>

790 and CD8<sup>

791+</sup>

792 T cells at 4&#x02013;8-&#x003bc;M concentrations by increasing reactive oxygen species (ROS) production as well as caspase 3 and 8 activity [<a href="#R20" rid="R20" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461500">

79320</a>

794].</p>

795<p id="P8">

796Ajulemic acid, a side-chain synthetic analog of &#x00394;(8)-THC-11-oic acid, has been shown to induce apoptosis in human peripheral blood T lymphocytes via the intrinsic pathway at concentrations of 1, 3 and 10 &#x003bc;M [<a href="#R21" rid="R21" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461509">

79721</a>

798]. In addition, the use of synthetic CB2 agonist JWH-015 treatment <em>

799in vitro</em>

800 led to cell death via both the death-receptor pathway and the intrinsic pathway. When JWH-015 was administered <em>

801in vivo</em>

802, the antigen-specific response to <em>

803Staphylococcal</em>

804 enterotoxin A was inhibited significantly [<a href="#R22" rid="R22" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461376">

80522</a>

806].</p>

807<p id="P9" class="p p-last">

808It is important to note that, unlike in immune cells, cannabinoids can protect from apoptosis in nontransformed cells of the CNS, which can play a protective role in autoimmune conditions such as multiple sclerosis. Cannabinoids protect against apoptosis of oligodendrocytes via CB1 and CB2 receptors, by signaling through the PI3K/AKT pathway. <em>

809In vivo</em>

810 and <em>

811in vitro</em>

812 exposure to arachidonyl-2-ethylamide (ACEA) and WIN55,212-12 protected the cells, while pretreatment with CB1 receptor antagonist SR141716A and CB2 receptor antagonist SR144528 blocked the action of these cannabinoids [<a href="#R23" rid="R23" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461400">

81323</a>

814]. In a different study by Jackson <em>

815et al.</em>

816, 3D mouse brain aggregate cell cultures were compared between wild-type mice and CB1 receptor knockout mice. IFN-&#x003b3; treatment led to decrease in the neurofilament-H expression in knockout cultures but not in wild-type cultures. In addition, caspase 3 activation was higher in knockout cultures, indicating a protective role of CB1 in neuronal cells [<a href="#R24" rid="R24" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461517">

81724</a>

818].</p>

819</div>

820<div id="S2" class="tsec sec">

821<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S2title">

822Cannabinoid action on cytokines</h2>

823<p id="P10" class="p p-first">

824Cytokines are the signaling proteins synthesized and secreted by immune cells upon stimulation. They are the modulating factors that balance initiation and resolution of <strong>

825inflammation</strong>

826. One of the possible mechanisms of immune control by cannabinoids during inflammation is the dys-regulation of cytokine production by immune cells and disruption of the well-regulated immune response [<a href="#R25" rid="R25" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461491">

82725</a>

828]. Furthermore, cannabinoids may affect immune responses and host resistance by perturbing the balance between the cytokines produced by T-helper subsets, Th1 and Th2. <em>

829In vitro</em>

830 studies were performed to compare the effect of THC and cannabinol on cytokine production by human T, B, CD8<sup>

831+</sup>

832, NK and eosinophilic cell lines. However, the results were variable, depending on the cell line and the concentration used [<a href="#R26" rid="R26" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461445">

83326</a>

834]. Both pro-inflammatory and anti-inflammatory effects of THC were demonstrated in this study, proposing that different cell populations have varied thresholds of response to cannabinoids. Generally, TNF-&#x003b1;, GM-CSF and IFN-&#x003b3; levels decreased with drug treatment. Interestingly, while the anti-inflammatory cytokine IL-10 decreased following THC treatment, there was an increase in the proinflammatory cytokine IL-8. In other studies, cannabinoid CP55,940 at nanomolar concentrations was shown to have a stimulatory effect on several cytokines in the human promyelocytic cell line HL-60 [<a href="#R27" rid="R27" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461506">

83527</a>

836]. At the molecular level, THC has also been shown to inhibit LPS-stimulated mRNA expression of IL-1&#x003b1;, IL-1&#x003b2;, IL-6 and TNF-&#x003b1; in cultured rat microglial cells; however, the effect was independent of the cannabinoid receptors [<a href="#R28" rid="R28" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461423">

83728</a>

838]. In a different study, mice were challenged with <em>

839Corynebacterium parvum, in vivo</em>

840, following the administration of the synthetic cannabinoids WIN55,212-2 and HU210. The animals were then challenged with LPS. The results showed decreased levels of TNF-&#x003b1; and IL-12 but increased levels of IL-10 in the serum [<a href="#R29" rid="R29" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461502">

84129</a>

842]. This effect was shown to be CB1 receptor dependent.</p>

843<p id="P11">

844During chronic inflammation, IL-6 suppression can decrease tissue injury [<a href="#R30" rid="R30" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461462">

84530</a>

846]. AjA has been reported to prevent joint-tissue injury in animal models of adjuvant arthritis [<a href="#R31" rid="R31" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461358">

84731</a>

848]. Recent studies showed that addition of AjA to human monocyte-derived macrophages <em>

849in vitro</em>

850 reduced the secretion of IL-6 from activated cells, suggesting that AjA may have a value for treatment of joint inflammation in patients with systemic lupus erythematosus (SLE), rheumatoid arthritis (RA) and osteoarthritis [<a href="#R32" rid="R32" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461352">

85132</a>

852]. It has been observed that the CB2 agonist HU-308 attenuated the hepatic ischemia/reperfusion injury by decreasing the levels of TNF-&#x003b1;, MIP-1&#x003b1; and MIP-2 in the serum and in liver homogenates [<a href="#R33" rid="R33" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461404">

85333</a>

854]. Recent <em>

855in vitro</em>

856 studies have also shown the potent anti-inflammatory effect of synthetic cannabinoids (CP55,940 and WIN55,212-2). Both CP55,940 and WIN55,212-2 downregulated IL-6 and IL-8 cytokine production from IL-1&#x003b2;-stimulated rheumatoid fibroblast-like synoviocytes (FLS), via a non-CB1/CB2-mediated mechanism [<a href="#R34" rid="R34" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461363">

85734</a>

858].</p>

859<p id="P12" class="p p-last">

860Endocannabinoids have also been reported to affect the cytokine biology of various cell systems. Antiproliferative effects of endocannabinoids on cancer cell lines are well established and are discussed in the later section of the review. However, AEA has also been reported to increase cytokine-induced proliferation. Mouse bone marrow cells, when cultured in the presence of IL-3 and AEA, were observed to produce more hematopoietic colonies than with IL-3 alone [<a href="#R35" rid="R35" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461416">

86135</a>

862]. Significant suppression of IL-2 expression by 2-AG and the nonhydrolyzable 2-AG ether was observed in leukocytes via activation of peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor-&#x003b3; (PPAR-&#x003b3;) [<a href="#R36" rid="R36" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461410">

86336</a>

864]. Furthermore, in undifferentiated and macrophage-like differentiated HL-60 cells, 2-AG induced CB2-dependent acceleration in the production of IL-8 [<a href="#R37" rid="R37" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461504">

86537</a>

866]. In Theiler&#x02019;s virus immune-mediated demyelinating disease, inactivation of endocannabinoids through the use of two selective inhibitors of their transport; (<em>

867R</em>

868)-<em>

869N</em>

870-oleoyl-(1&#x02032;-hydroxybenzyl)-2&#x02032;-ethanolamine] (OMDM2) and [(<em>

871S</em>

872)-<em>

873N</em>

874-oleoyl-(1&#x02032;-hydroxybenzyl)-2&#x02032;-ethanolamine (OMDM1) led to decreased production of the proinflammatory cytokines IL-1&#x003b2; and IL-12 [<a href="#R38" rid="R38" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461431">

87538</a>

876]. On a contrary note, cytokines have also been shown to affect the endocannabinoid system. IL-12 and IFN-&#x003b3; have been shown to reduce FAAH activity and protein expression of FAAH, whereas IL-4 or IL-10 stimulated FAAH activity [<a href="#R39" rid="R39" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461458">

87739</a>

878]. <a href="/pmc/articles/PMC2828614/table/T2/" target="table" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="T2" rid-ob="ob-T2" co-legend-rid="">

879<span>

880Table 2</span>

881</a>

882 provides a summary of the effect of cannabinoids on cytokines and chemokines in various cell models [<a href="#R26" rid="R26" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461424">

88326</a>

884,<a href="#R28" rid="R28" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461370">

88528</a>

886,<a href="#R29" rid="R29" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461515">

88729</a>

888,<a href="#R32" rid="R32" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461433">

88932</a>

890&#x02013;<a href="#R34" rid="R34" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461474">

89134</a>

892,<a href="#R37" rid="R37" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461383">

89337</a>

894,<a href="#R40" rid="R40" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461393">

89540</a>

896,<a href="#R41" rid="R41" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461505">

89741</a>

898].</p>

1071<div id="S3" class="tsec sec">

1072<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S3title">

1073Cannabinoids &#x00026; multiple sclerosis</h2>

1074<p id="P13" class="p p-first">

1075Multiple sclerosis (MS) is an autoimmune disorder that is mediated by myelin-specific self-reactive T cells, macrophages/microglial cells and astrocytes [<a href="#R3" rid="R3" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461411">

10763</a>

1077,<a href="#R42" rid="R42" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461427">

107842</a>

1079]. The action of these cells leads to the demyelination of nerve fibers and axons in the CNS of humans and results in many signs and symptoms, such as muscle spasms, tremor, ataxia, weakness or paralysis, constipation and loss of bladder control [<a href="#R42" rid="R42" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461482">

108042</a>

1081]. There is both anecdotal and clinical evidence to show the effectiveness of cannabinoids in the treatment of MS. In 1994, a survey of 112 MS patients (57 men and 55 women) from the USA and UK was conducted; all of the patients were self-medicating with a form of cannabis. The results of the survey showed that cannabis use improved symptoms such as spasticity, pain, tremor and depression in more than 90% of patients. In eight different clinical studies, MS patients have also reported the benefits of THC (administered via ingestion, inhalation, injection or rectal suppository), cannabis (administered via ingestion or inhalation) and the cannabinoid receptor agonist Nabilone<sup>

1082&#x02122;</sup>

1083, (administered via ingestion) in treating spasticity, pain, tremor and ataxia [<a href="#R43" rid="R43" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461497">

108443</a>

1085]. Use of cannabinoids also improved objective test results such as hand-writing tests and bladder control tests [<a href="#R43" rid="R43" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461417">

108643</a>

1087,<a href="#R44" rid="R44" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461496">

108844</a>

1089]. In general, cannabinoids are useful in treating MS because they have neuroprotective as well as immunosuppressive properties [<a href="#R44" rid="R44" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461390">

109044</a>

1091,<a href="#R45" rid="R45" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461513">

109245</a>

1093]. In this section, we will focus on the latter and discuss the action of endogenous, natural and synthetic cannabinoids on immune cells within the CNS during MS.</p>

1094<p id="P14">

1095The destruction of the blood&#x02013;brain barrier in MS is initiated by myelin-specific self-reactive T cells. Infiltration of these cells into the spinal cord and CNS, and their subsequent activation, leads to the elimination of the myelin sheath around the nerves and axons [<a href="#R46" rid="R46" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461438">

109646</a>

1097,<a href="#R47" rid="R47" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461405">

109847</a>

1099]. The myelin- specific T cells are usually CD4<sup>

1100+</sup>

1101, IL-2R<sup>

1102+</sup>

1103 or MHCII-restricted Th1 cells and they secrete proinflammatory cytokines such as IFN-&#x003b3; and TNF-&#x003b1; [<a href="#R47" rid="R47" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461379">

110447</a>

1105]. More recently, Th17 cells have been shown to be involved in the pathogenesis of MS [<a href="#R48" rid="R48" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461430">

110648</a>

1107,<a href="#R49" rid="R49" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461420">

110849</a>

1109]. One mechanism of immunosuppression by cannabinoids is the induction of apoptosis and Sanchez <em>

1110et al.</em>

1111 demonstrated that WIN55,212-2 blocks a passive form of experimental authoimmune encephalomyelitis (EAE) by inducing apoptosis in encephalitogenic cells through partial activation of the CB2 receptor [<a href="#R50" rid="R50" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461483">

111250</a>

1113]. A CB1-mediated suppressive pathway has also been shown in myelin-specific T cells [<a href="#R24" rid="R24" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461441">

111424</a>

1115]. This study demonstrated that <em>

1116ex vivo</em>

1117 WIN55,212-2 inhibited T-cell recall response to myelin oligodendrocyte glycoprotein (MOG) peptide, as well as decreasing IL-2, IFN-&#x003b3; and TNF-&#x003b1; production by MOG-activated T cells. Other synthetic cannabinoids, such as JWH-015 and ACEA, also decreased the number of CD4<sup>

1118+</sup>

1119 infiltrates in the spinal cord of Theiler&#x02019;s murine encephalomyelitis virus (TMEV)-infected mice [<a href="#R51" rid="R51" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_809316601">

112051</a>

1121]. Mestre <em>

1122et al.</em>

1123 showed that decreased infiltration of CD4<sup>

1124+</sup>

1125 T cells upon WIN55,212-2 treatment in EAE mice is due to decreased intercellular and vascular cell adhesion molecules (ICAM-1 and VCAM-1) expression by endothelial cells. Another novel finding of this study demonstrated that WIN55,212-2 exerted its effects by acting through nuclear receptor PPAR-&#x003b3; [<a href="#R52" rid="R52" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461415">

112652</a>

1127].</p>

1128<p id="P15">

1129Microglial cells are the macrophages of the CNS and, during MS, they mediate tissue injury in two main ways: antigen presentation and cytokine/chemokine secretion [<a href="#R51" rid="R51" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_809316599">

113051</a>

1131,<a href="#R52" rid="R52" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461463">

113252</a>

1133]. In the initial stages of inflammation, after activation, microglial cells present antigens to myelin-specific T cells, which results in the activation and proliferation of Th1 lineage cells. Arevalo-Martin <em>

1134et al.</em>

1135 demonstrated that cannabinoid agonists WIN55,212-2, ACEA or JWH-015 inhibited the activation of microglial cells by TMEV [<a href="#R51" rid="R51" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_809316600">

113651</a>

1137]. The investigators confirmed this finding by studying the morphology of the cells (reactive vs resting) as well as by immunohistochemistry. They showed that, after TMEV activation, MHCII molecules co-localized with Mac-1 in the spinal cord sections; however, after 1-day treatment with various cannabinoid agonists, MHCII expression almost disappeared. During this initial stage, co-stimulatory molecule expression, such as that of CD40, also increased and resulted in TNF-&#x003b1; production via the MAPK and JAK/STAT pathways. Ehrhart and colleagues demonstrated that selective stimulation of the CB2 receptor with JWH-015 on murine microglial cells decreased CD40 expression upon IFN-&#x003b3; activation. This inhibition in CD40 levels translated into decreased JAK/STAT phosphorylation, and decreased TNF-&#x003b1; and nitric oxide production [<a href="#R53" rid="R53" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461367">

113853</a>

1139].</p>

1140<p id="P16">

1141In the later stages of disease, microglial cells secrete IL-12, IL-13 and IL-23, nitric oxide and glutamate and contribute to myelin sheath destruction. IL-12 drives the proliferation of Th1 cells while IL-23 is important in the maintenance of Th17 cells. A recent study by Correa <em>

1142et al.</em>

1143 showed that the endogenous cannabinoid AEA inhibited the expression of IL-12 as well as IL-23 in LPS/IFN-&#x003b3;-activated human and murine microglia. This inhibition of cytokine production occurred via activation of CB2 and signaling through ERK1/2 and JNK pathways [<a href="#R54" rid="R54" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461512">

114454</a>

1145]. Palazuelos <em>

1146et al.</em>

1147 also showed that the CB2 receptor is involved in myeloid progenitor trafficking, which is necessary for microglia replenishment and activation during MS. Their studies demonstrated that <em>

1148CB2</em>

1149<sup>

1150&#x02212;/&#x02212;</sup>

1151 mice had exacerbated EAE symptoms and CD34<sup>

1152+</sup>

1153 myeloid progenitor cells had greatly infiltrated into the spinal cords of these animals. As an explanation for the mechanism, they showed that, in the bone marrow, CB2 receptor manipulation with HU-308 increased the expression of chemokines and their receptors (CCL2, CCL3, CCL5, CCR1 and CCR2), which are important in trafficking of progenitor cells into the neuroinflamed tissue [<a href="#R55" rid="R55" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461356">

115455</a>

1155].</p>

1156<p id="P17" class="p p-last">

1157Cannabinoids also exert their immunosuppressive effects on astrocytes. Astrocytes make up 60&#x02013;70% of brain cells in the CNS and play important roles in neuronal growth, neuronal signaling, glucose metabolism and glutamate removal [<a href="#R54" rid="R54" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461485">

115854</a>

1159]. During disease progression, astrocytes are activated to secrete cytokines, chemokines and nitric oxide, thereby contributing to the overall inflammatory response. Because astrocytes express both CB1 and CB2 receptors, several studies investigated the inhibitory role of cannabinoids on this cell population in the context of MS. One study investigated the effects of AEA on TMEV-activated primary murine astrocytes. This study showed that AEA stimulated astrocytes and triggered the production of IL-6 in a CB1-mediated pathway [<a href="#R56" rid="R56" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461464">

116056</a>

1161]. The precise role of IL-6 in the CNS is still unclear; however, it has been reported that IL-6 secretion potentiates neuronal growth factor production. In addition, IL-6 has been shown to inhibit TNF-&#x003b1; production by IFN-&#x003b3;/IL-1&#x003b2;-stimulated glial cells [<a href="#R57" rid="R57" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461470">

116257</a>

1163]. In a different study, Molina-Holgado and coworkers showed that AEA and the synthetic CB1 agonist CP-55940 inhibited nitric oxide production by LPS-stimulated astrocytes isolated from 1-day-old mice in a CB1-dependent manner [<a href="#R23" rid="R23" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461414">

116423</a>

1165]. In 2005, Sheng <em>

1166et al.</em>

1167 demonstrated that human fetal astrocytes express both CB1 and CB2 receptors and that treatment of IL-1&#x003b2;-stimulated astrocytes with WIN55,212-2 decreased inflammatory products including nitric oxide, TNF-&#x003b1;, CXCL10, CCL2 and CCL5 (<a href="/pmc/articles/PMC2828614/figure/F1/" target="figure" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="F1" rid-ob="ob-F1" co-legend-rid="lgnd_F1">

1168<span>

1169Figure 1</span>

1170</a>

1171) [<a href="#R54" rid="R54" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461439">

117254</a>

1173].</p>

1175<!--fig mode=article f1-->

1176<div class="fig iconblock whole_rhythm" id="F1" co-legend-rid="lgnd_F1">

1198<!--caption a8-->

1199<strong>

1200Cannabinoids and multiple sclerosis</strong>

1201<p id="__p2">

1202The three main cell types that are involved in demyelination of the nerve fibers and axons in the CNS include activated T-cells, microglia and astrocytes. In activated T-cells, treatment with WIN 55,212-12, AEA and JWH-015 has been shown to inhibit cytokine production, infiltration of cells into the spinal cord and <em>

1203in vitro</em>

1204 recall response to myelin oligodendrocyte glycoprotein by T-cells. Cannabinoids also inhibit the antigen presenting abilities of microglia by downregulating MHCII expression, costimulatory molecule CD40 expression, as well as cytokine secretion. Astrocytes, the major cell population in the brain, are also affected, as cannabinoid binding to the receptors leads to inhibition of inflammatory molecules, such as nitric oxide, cytokines and chemokines. In addition, anandamide binding leads to secretion of neural growth factor secretion and protection of the neurons in the CNS.</p>

1205<p id="__p3">

1206ACEA: Arachidonyl-2-ethylamide; NGF: Neuronal growth factor.</p>

1207</div>

1208</div>

1209</div>

1210</div>

1211<div id="S4" class="tsec sec">

1212<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S4title">

1213Cannabinoids &#x00026; colitis</h2>

1214<p id="P18" class="p p-first">

1215During inflammation, several different cellular pathways are activated in the intestinal tract, leading to a pathological state [<a href="#R58" rid="R58" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461434">

121658</a>

1217]. Functional CB1 receptor has been shown to be expressed in the human ileum and colon and the number of CB1-expressing cells was found to be significantly increased after inflammation [<a href="#R59" rid="R59" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461397">

121859</a>

1219,<a href="#R60" rid="R60" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461359">

122060</a>

1221]. A protective role for these CB1 receptors during inflammation has been shown in a study analyzing the role of the endogenous cannabinoid system in the development of experimental colitis in mice, induced by intrarectal 2,4-dinitrobenzene sulfonic acid (DNBS) treatment or oral dextran sodium sulfate (DSS) administration [<a href="#R59" rid="R59" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461428">

122259</a>

1223]. The DSS model, originally reported by Okayasu <em>

1224et al.,</em>

1225 has been used to investigate the role of leukocytes in the development of colitis [<a href="#R61" rid="R61" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461394">

122661</a>

1227]. Oral administration of 5% DSS in drinking water can induce acute colitis due to chemical injury in the colon. Furthermore, long-term DSS administration produces colorectal carcinoma, which is similar to the dysplasia&#x02013;carcinoma sequence seen in the course of cancer development in human ulcerative colitis [<a href="#R62" rid="R62" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461354">

122862</a>

1229]. On the other hand, intestinal inflammation induced by the intrarectal administration of DNBS has many of the characteristic features of Crohn&#x02019;s disease in humans, involving induction of an IL-12-driven inflammation with a massive Th1-mediated response [<a href="#R63" rid="R63" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461481">

123063</a>

1231]. The involvement of the endogenous cannabinoid system in the modulation of the acute phase of DNBS-induced colitis was further supported by the increased levels of transcripts coding for CB1 in wild-type mice after induction of inflammation. It was observed that genetic ablation of CB1 receptors rendered mice more sensitive to inflammatory insults. Furthermore, similar to results observed in CB1-deficient mice, pharmacological blockade of CB1 with the specific antagonist SR141716A led to a worsening of colitis [<a href="#R59" rid="R59" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461467">

123259</a>

1233]. The protective role of the endogenous cannabinoid system was observed 24 h after DNBS treatment and became more evident on days 2 and 3. However, increased spontaneous spiking activity of smooth muscle cell membrane in DNBS-treated colons from <em>

1234CB1</em>

1235<sup>

1236&#x02212;/&#x02212;</sup>

1237 mice was already visible 8 h after DNBS treatment, indicating that inflammation-induced irritation of smooth muscle occurred at an earlier stage than in wild-type mice. This gives further support to the notion that the endogenous cannabinoid system is protective against inflammatory changes. These data indicated that the activation of CB1 and the endogenous cannabinoid system is an early and important physiological step in self-protection of the colon against inflammation.</p>

1238<p id="P19">

1239Pharmacological stimulation of cannabinoid receptors with the potent agonist HU210 also induced a reduction of experimental colitis. It has been reported that cannabinoid receptor stimulation could have a beneficial effect on experimental colitis [<a href="#R64" rid="R64" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461403">

124064</a>

1241]. Intraperitoneal application of ACEA, a CB1-selective agonist, and JWH-133, a CB2-selective agonist, inhibited oil of mustard (OM)-induced colitis and subsequent symptoms such as induced distal colon weight gain, colon shrinkage, inflammatory damage, diarrhea and histological damage. This study demonstrated a role for CB2 activation in experimental colitis. The fact that both CB1 and CB2 agonists are active in colitis models lends additional support to the theory that signaling through cannabinoid receptors may mediate protective mechanisms in colitis.</p>

1242<p id="P20">

1243In the small intestine, the involvement of CB1 receptors in the control of intestinal motility during croton oil-induced inflammation was recently demonstrated. Izzo <em>

1244et al.</em>

1245 showed that pharmacological administration of cannabinoids is able to delay gastrointestinal transit in croton oil-treated mice [<a href="#R65" rid="R65" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461375">

124665</a>

1247]. It was further suggested that increased levels of CB1 receptor expression in inflamed jejuna may contribute to this protective effect. CB1 receptors were shown to modulate gastrointestinal motility during croton oil-induced inflammation in mice.</p>

1248<p id="P21">

1249Fatty acid amide hydrolase is the major enzyme involved in the degradation of several bioactive fatty amides, in particular anandamide [<a href="#R11" rid="R11" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461396">

125011</a>

1251], and its genetic deletion in mice leads to a strongly decreased ability to degrade this endocannabinoid and an increase of anandamide levels in several tissues [<a href="#R66" rid="R66" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461468">

125266</a>

1253]. FAAH-deficient mice showed significant protection against DNBS treatment. However, because anandamide is believed to act not only through cannabinoid receptors but also through other targets, including the peripheral vanilloid receptor TRPV1 [<a href="#R67" rid="R67" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461377">

125467</a>

1255], the decreased inflammation in <em>

1256FAAH</em>

1257<sup>

1258&#x02212;/&#x02212;</sup>

1259 mice could also be due to the activation of targets other than cannabinoid receptors.</p>

1260<p id="P22" class="p p-last">

1261In conclusion, cannabinoids have been shown to regulate the tissue response to excessive inflammation in the colon, mediated by both dampening smooth-muscular irritation caused by inflammation and suppressing proinflammatory cytokines, thus controlling the cellular pathways leading to inflammatory responses. These results strongly suggest that modulation of the physiological activity of the cannabinoid system during colonic inflammation might be a promising therapeutic tool for the treatment of several diseases characterized by inflammation of the GI tract.</p>

1262</div>

1263<div id="S5" class="tsec sec">

1264<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S5title">

1265Cannabinoid system &#x00026; liver injury</h2>

1266<p id="P23" class="p p-first">

1267During the past few years, awareness of the cannabinoid system in the pathophysiology of liver disease has gained momentum. Both CB1 and CB2 receptors have been shown to be upregulated in the early stages of liver injury [<a href="#R68" rid="R68" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461362">

126868</a>

1269&#x02013;<a href="#R72" rid="R72" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461511">

127072</a>

1271]. Although embryonic liver has been shown to express CB2 receptor mRNA, adult liver hepatocytes and endothelial cells displayed only a faint physiological level of expression of CB1 receptors and were shown to produce low levels of endocannabinoids. CB1 receptors have been found to be upregulated in the vascular endothelium and in myofibroblasts located in fibrotic bands of cirrhotic livers in human and rodents [<a href="#R72" rid="R72" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461385">

127272</a>

1273]. CB2 receptors are also expressed in myofibroblasts, inflammatory cells and biliary epithelial cells [<a href="#R69" rid="R69" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461409">

127469</a>

1275]. There has been growing evidence in recent years to suggest that endocannabinoids may regulate the pathophysiology of liver diseases, including both acute forms of hepatic injury, liver fibrosis and cirrhosis. The endocannabinoids are found in low levels in normal liver, which may be due to high levels of expression of FAAH, which is responsible for the breakdown of AEA [<a href="#R11" rid="R11" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461384">

127611</a>

1277]. The levels of AEA have been shown to increase in the liver and serum during acute hepatitis and fatty liver disease [<a href="#R70" rid="R70" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461380">

127870</a>

1279]. In fatty liver, the increase in AEA results from decreased ability of FAAH to degrade AEA. Together, the above studies suggest that endocannabinoids and their receptors may play a critical role in regulating liver fibrogenesis; therefore, targeting the cannabinoid receptors may serve as a novel tool to prevent and treat liver injury.</p>

1280<p id="P24">

1281While the mechanisms of inflammatory liver injury are unclear, they are accompanied by infiltration of activated polymorphonuclear leukocytes, activation of Kupffer cells, production of proinflammatory cytokines and generation of ROS. Many recent studies indicated strongly the increased upregulation of the endocannabinoid system during liver diseases involving hepatocyte injury, inflammation, fibrogenesis, hepatic encephalopathy, cirrhotic cardiomyopathy and portal hypertension [<a href="#R73" rid="R73" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461392">

128273</a>

1283]. The role of hepatic expression of anandamide and 2-AG is apparent in hepatic ischemia-reperfusion (I/R) injury, in which their levels are significantly increased, correlating with the extent of liver damage. Moreover, pretreatment of mice with JWH-133, a CB2 receptor agonist, was shown to decrease the degree of liver tissue injury and inflammatory cell infiltration and decrease serum levels of cytokines, chemokines and adhesion molecules [<a href="#R74" rid="R74" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461369">

128474</a>

1285]. Furthermore, <em>

1286CB2</em>

1287<sup>

1288&#x02212;/&#x02212;</sup>

1289 receptor mice were shown to develop greater inflammation and I/R-induced liver damage than their wild-type counterparts. The data also highlights the protective role of CB2 receptor activation in the inflammatory response associated with chronic liver diseases such as viral hepatitis and alcoholic or nonalcoholic fatty liver diseases.</p>

1290<p id="P25">

1291Viral hepatitis, alcohol abuse and nonalcoholic fatty liver are some of the conditions that can induce chronic liver injury and inflammation, leading to activation of fibrogenesis as a wound-healing mechanism. However, persistence of fibrogenic stimuli can enhance deposition of the extracellular matrix by hepatic myofibroblasts, thus disrupting normal liver architecture and, ultimately, leading to cirrhosis and liver failure. CB1 and CB2 receptors are shown to be markedly upregulated in cirrhotic human liver samples, demonstrating the impact of endocannabinoids in liver fibrogenesis. In addition, increases in circulating levels of anadamide and hepatic 2-AG have also been reported in cirrhosis and liver fibrosis, respectively [<a href="#R73" rid="R73" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461407">

129273</a>

1293]. <em>

1294CB2</em>

1295<sup>

1296&#x02212;/&#x02212;</sup>

1297 mice exposed to CCl4 showed enhanced liver fibrosis when compared with wild-type mice, thereby suggesting a protective role for CB2 receptor activation in liver fibrosis. By contrast, activation of CB1 receptors was found to promote profibrotic response [<a href="#R72" rid="R72" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461387">

129872</a>

1299]. The pharmacological inactivation of CB1 with rimonabant<sup>

1300&#x000ae;</sup>

1301 (SR141716) results in the reduction of obesity and hepatic steatosis in rodents [<a href="#R75" rid="R75" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461440">

130275</a>

1303], demonstrating that CB1 and CB2 receptors exert opposite effects on liver fibrosis and further suggesting that endocannabinoid system regulates both pro- and anti-fibrogenic responses in the liver. Further effects of the endocannabinoids have also been shown to be receptor independent. AEA and 2-AG have been shown to induce necrosis and apoptosis, respectively, in activated hepatic stellate cells, through increased generation of ROS [<a href="#R76" rid="R76" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461456">

130476</a>

1305].</p>

1306<p id="P26">

1307The abuse of cannabis has been shown to promote liver fibrosis in patients with chronic hepatitis C, indicating that cannabinoids may exacerbate liver fibrogenesis and that CB1 receptor antagonists may play a role as anti-fibrosing molecules [<a href="#R71" rid="R71" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461422">

130871</a>

1309]. However, an alternative explanation could be that marijuana can trigger immunosuppression. For example, CB2 activation in immune cells can trigger apoptosis and this, in turn, can have an immunosuppressive effect in patients with hepatitis C. As such patients require immunocompetent cells to keep hepatitis under control, chronic marijuana abuse may promote fibrogenesis through the activation of CB2 and consequent suppression of antiviral immunity [<a href="#R77" rid="R77" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461453">

131077</a>

1311].</p>

1312<p id="P27">

1313Endocannabinoids may also regulate liver cirrhosis by acting as mediators of vascular and cardiac functions. Endocannabinoids can trigger vasorelaxation, while an upregulated CB1-mediated cannabinoid tone causes enhanced mesenteric vasodialation leading to portal hypertension [<a href="#R73" rid="R73" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461489">

131473</a>

1315,<a href="#R75" rid="R75" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461378">

131675</a>

1317]. A recent <em>

1318in vivo</em>

1319 study by Batkai <em>

1320et al.</em>

1321 in rats with CCl<sub>

13224</sub>

1323-induced cirrhosis, indicated that increased local production of AEA mediated the inhibition of &#x003b2;-adrenergic responsiveness. Further improvement in contractile function of isolated papillary muscles was observed following treatment with AM251, a CB1 receptor antagonist, suggesting therapeutic potential against cirrhotic cardiomyopathy [<a href="#R75" rid="R75" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461413">

132475</a>

1325].</p>

1326<p id="P28" class="p p-last">

1327There are limited, but reliable, data on the neuroprotective role of the endocannabinoid system in hepatic encephalopathy. It has been demonstrated in a murine model that, during fulminant hepatic failure, levels of 2-AG in the brain are elevated, potentially as a response to liver damage. The administration of the CB2 endogenous ligand 2-AG, an antagonist of CB1 receptor SR141716A or an agonist of CB2 receptor HU308, resulted in a marked improvement in neurological score. Thus, influencing the endocannabinoid system with exogenous cannabinoid derivates specific for the CB1 or CB2 receptor may have a beneficial therapeutic effect on neurological dysfunction in liver diseases [<a href="#R78" rid="R78" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461398">

132878</a>

1329]. Recently, we noted that both exogenous and endogenous cannabinoids protected mice from concanavalin-A (ConA)-induced acute hepatitis, a model that mimics viral or autoimmune hepatitis, in which T cells play a critical role in triggering liver injury. We found that administration of a single dose of THC or anandamide could ameliorate Con-A-induced hepatitis. We found that this effect was mediated through multiple pathways, including suppression of pro-inflammatory cytokines, induction of apoptosis in activated T cells and induction of forkhead helix transcription factor p3(Foxp3)<sup>

1330+</sup>

1331 Treg cells [<a href="#R79" rid="R79" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461488">

133279</a>

1333]. This overwhelming evidence shows that the cannabinoid system must play a major role in the pathophysiology of various liver diseases and its therapeutic potential should be exploited for the treatment of chronic liver injuries (<a href="/pmc/articles/PMC2828614/figure/F2/" target="figure" class="fig-table-link figpopup" rid-figpopup="F2" rid-ob="ob-F2" co-legend-rid="lgnd_F2">

1334<span>

1335Figure 2</span>

1336</a>

1337).</p>

1338<!--fig ft0-->

1339<!--fig mode=article f1-->

1340<div class="fig iconblock whole_rhythm" id="F2" co-legend-rid="lgnd_F2">

1341<a href="/pmc/articles/PMC2828614/figure/F2/" target="figure" rid-figpopup="F2" rid-ob="ob-F2">

1362<p id="__p4">

1363Endocannabinoids, CB1 antagonists and CB2 agonists as potential drugs for the treatment of liver injury.</p>

1364</div>

1366</div>

1367</div>

1368<div id="S6" class="tsec sec">

1369<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S6title">

1370Cannabinoids &#x00026; RA</h2>

1371<p id="P29" class="p p-first">

1372Rheumatoid arthritis is a chronic inflammatory disease that affects approximately 1% of the human population and is manifested in joint destruction, deformity and loss of function associated with joint stiffness, pain, swelling and tenderness [<a href="#R80" rid="R80" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461381">

137380</a>

1374]. The major immune cell populations involved in joint injury are macrophages, T cells, fibroblast-like synoviocytes and DCs. Major cytokines are TNF-&#x003b1; and IL-1 [<a href="#R80" rid="R80" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461371">

137580</a>

1376,<a href="#R81" rid="R81" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461421">

137781</a>

1378]. Cannabinoids and their anti-inflammatory properties have been studied in animal models of RA and on human cells from RA patients and these studies demonstrate the anti-arthritic properties of these natural plant compounds [<a href="#R32" rid="R32" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461451">

137932</a>

1380, <a href="#R82" rid="R82" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461419">

138182</a>

1382&#x02013;<a href="#R84" rid="R84" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461499">

138384</a>

1384]. Interestingly, most of the studies on RA and cannabinoids focus on the use of nonpsychoactive cannabinoids.</p>

1385<p id="P30">

1386CBD is the major nonpsychoactive component of the cannabis plant and its protective effect has been shown in murine collagen-induced arthritis [<a href="#R85" rid="R85" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461391">

138785</a>

1388]. In this study, the authors showed that daily oral (5 mg/kg) or intra-peritoneal (25 mg/kg) administration of CBD inhibited disease progression. Furthermore, the study demonstrated that CBD-treated mice had less proliferation in <em>

1389ex vivo</em>

1390-activated draining lymph node cells, decreased levels of IFN-&#x003b3; secreted by the activated lymph node cells and diminished TNF-&#x003b1; production by knee synovial cells. Sumariwalla <em>

1391et al.</em>

1392 used another synthetic nonpsychoactive cannabinoid, HU-320, and demonstrated that this compound improved already established arthritis in mice [<a href="#R86" rid="R86" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461361">

139386</a>

1394]. Lymph node cells from HU-320-treated mice showed decreased proliferative responses when the cells from 7-day post-inflammation mice were incubated with collagen II.</p>

1395<p id="P31">

1396Furthermore, AjA has been shown to inhibit IL-1&#x003b2; production by human monocytes isolated from RA patients [<a href="#R82" rid="R82" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461443">

139782</a>

1398]. In this study, the investigators isolated peripheral blood monocytes (PBMs) as well as synovial fluid monocytes (SFMs) from normal and RA patients. The isolated cells were pretreated with 0&#x02013;30 &#x003bc;M AjA and were then stimulated with LPS. The results showed that AjA treatment decreased IL-1&#x003b2; production in both PBMs and SFMs, but did not affect TNF-&#x003b1; production. In a different study, Parker <em>

1399et al.</em>

1400 demonstrated that AjA reduced IL-6 secretion by activated human monocyte-derived-macrophages [<a href="#R32" rid="R32" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461426">

140132</a>

1402].</p>

1403<p id="P32" class="p p-last">

1404AjA also exerts its immunomodulatory effects by inducing apoptosis in mature osteoclast-like cells and, therefore, protecting the host from osteoclastogenesis. George <em>

1405et al.</em>

1406 demonstrated that treatment of osteoclast-like cells with 15 and 30 &#x003bc;M of AjA led to induction of caspase 3 and 8 in these cells, resulting in apoptosis and protection from osteoclastogenesis [<a href="#R83" rid="R83" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461452">

140783</a>

1408].</p>

1409</div>

1410<div id="S7" class="tsec sec">

1411<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S7title">

1412Cannabinoids &#x00026; cancers with inflammatory components</h2>

1413<p id="P33" class="p p-first-last">

1414The role of inflammation in evolution of certain types of cancer has been strongly suggested, linking the inflammatory response to 15&#x02013;20% of all deaths from cancer worldwide [<a href="#R84" rid="R84" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461412">

141584</a>

1416]. The hallmarks of cancer-related inflammation include the presence of inflammatory cells in tumor tissue, and the regulation of tumor growth, metastasis and angiogenesis by inflammatory mediators (e.g., chemokines, cytokines and prostaglandins). The connection between inflammation and cancer is now generally accepted and nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drugs have been shown to reduce varied cancer risk. Use of these drugs reduces colon cancer risk by 40&#x02013;50% and is indicated to be preventative for lung, esophagus and stomach cancer [<a href="#R87" rid="R87" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461471">

141787</a>

1418]. Hence, inflammation can be considered as a therapeutic opportunity in certain types of cancer. Recent applications of cannabinoids have been extended as antitumor agents [<a href="#R1" rid="R1" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461510">

14191</a>

1420,<a href="#R88" rid="R88" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461459">

142188</a>

1422], which relies on their ability to inhibit tumor angiogenesis [<a href="#R89" rid="R89" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461437">

142389</a>

1424] or induce direct apoptosis or cell cycle arrest in neoplastic cells [<a href="#R89" rid="R89" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461480">

142589</a>

1426&#x02013;<a href="#R92" rid="R92" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461368">

142792</a>

1428]. A focus on the antiproliferative effects of these compounds in various tumors, such as breast and prostate cancers, pheochromocytoma and malignant gliomas, has been proposed [<a href="#R1" rid="R1" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461507">

14291</a>

1430,<a href="#R92" rid="R92" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461357">

143192</a>

1432&#x02013;<a href="#R94" rid="R94" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461399">

143394</a>

1434]. Our laboratory reported that, <em>

1435in vitro</em>

1436, THC and other cannabinoids could induce apoptosis in transformed murine and human T cells [<a href="#R95" rid="R95" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461457">

143795</a>

1438], including primary acute lymphoblastic human leukemia cells. Furthermore, the treatment of mice bearing a T-cell leukemia with THC could cure approximately 25% of the mice [<a href="#R95" rid="R95" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461386">

143995</a>

1440]. We further showed that treatment with THC caused interruption of the MAPK/ERK kinase/ERK signaling module that was required for apoptotic lethality [<a href="#R96" rid="R96" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461498">

144196</a>

1442,<a href="#R97" rid="R97" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461447">

144397</a>

1444]. The role of endocannabinoids as potential endogenous tumor growth inhibitors has been suggested in a study where it was observed that levels of both AEA and 2-AG were higher in precancerous polyps than in fully developed carcinomas in the colon [<a href="#R98" rid="R98" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461425">

144598</a>

1446]. Recent <em>

1447in vivo</em>

1448 studies proposed that selective targeting of CB2 receptors resulted in colorectal tumor growth inhibition via apoptosis, which was mediated through the stimulation of ceramide [<a href="#R98" rid="R98" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461408">

144998</a>

1450]. In a xenograft model of thyroid cancer, substances that blocked endocannabinoid degradation also increased the levels of AEA and 2-AG in the tissue and reduced tumor growth [<a href="#R99" rid="R99" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461435">

145199</a>

1452]. Various attempts have been made to inactivate cannabinoid-degrading enzymes, thereby increasing the local concentration of endocannabinoids at the tumor cell surface. This leads to anti-tumor effects of CB receptor signaling in various cancer types, such as thyroid, brain and prostate cancer [<a href="#R99" rid="R99" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461484">

145399</a>

1454&#x02013;<a href="#R103" rid="R103" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461487">

1455103</a>

1456]. Although the majority of the effects of cannabinoids are CB receptor mediated, AEA has been shown to induce its effects on cancerous cells by interacting with TRPV1 receptor [<a href="#R104" rid="R104" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461454">

1457104</a>

1458,<a href="#R105" rid="R105" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461402">

1459105</a>

1460] or cholesterol-rich lipid rafts [<a href="#R106" rid="R106" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461355">

1461106</a>

1462]. Furthermore, it has been reported that signaling pathways are differentially regulated by cannabinoids in normal cells versus cancer cells. In malignancies, such as thyroid cancer, lymphoma, melanoma, pancreas and breast cancer, the levels of cannabinoid receptors are often higher in the tumor compared with normal cells of the same origin, resulting in increased sensitivity to cannabinoids in the malignancies [<a href="#R89" rid="R89" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461478">

146389</a>

1464,<a href="#R107" rid="R107" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461476">

1465107</a>

1466&#x02013;<a href="#R110" rid="R110" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461490">

1467110</a>

1468]. Moreover, many animal studies have reported antiproliferative and pro-apoptotic effects of cannabinoids on tumor cells but not on normal tissue [<a href="#R89" rid="R89" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461492">

146989</a>

1470,<a href="#R91" rid="R91" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461442">

147191</a>

1472]. Thus, the role of the cannabinoid system in cancer indicates that this system is involved in regulating many of the functions that are essential in cancer development.</p>

1473</div>

1474<div id="S8" class="tsec sec">

1475<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S8title">

1476Cannabinoids &#x00026; other inflammatory diseases</h2>

1477<p id="P34" class="p p-first">

1478Allergic asthma is a complex inflammatory disorder characterized by airway hyper-responsiveness, elevated serum IgE, recruitment of eosinophils into the lung and mucus hypersecretion by goblet cells [<a href="#R111" rid="R111" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461514">

1479111</a>

1480]. Murine models of allergic airway disease, employing ovalbumin (OVA) as an aeroallergen, indicated that CD4<sup>

1481+</sup>

1482 Th2 cells (IL-4, IL-5 and IL-13) played a pivotal role in the pathophysiology of the allergic airway response [<a href="#R112" rid="R112" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461472">

1483112</a>

1484]. Intraperitoneal administration of THC or cannabinol (CBN) in OVA-sensitized and challenged A/J mice led to attenuation of serum IgE, IL-2, IL-4, IL-5 and IL-13 mRNA expression and decreased allergen-induced mucus production, indicating that cannabinoid-based compounds may represent a novel class of therapeutic agents for the treatment of allergic airway diseases [<a href="#R113" rid="R113" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461503">

1485113</a>

1486]. While most studies have shown that cannabinoids, such as THC, facilitate a Th1 to Th2 cytokine switch, as discussed previously, it is surprising that cannabinoids can also suppress allergic asthma triggered primarily by Th2 cytokines. It is possible that THC may affect other cells such as DCs and B cells directly in this model. Previous findings indicated that aerosolized THC was capable of causing significant bronchodilatation with minimal systemic side effects, but had a local irritating effect on the airways [<a href="#R114" rid="R114" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461382">

1487114</a>

1488]. Further bronchodilator effects of cannabinoids administered orally or by aerosol to asthmatic patients have also been reported [<a href="#R115" rid="R115" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461455">

1489115</a>

1490,<a href="#R116" rid="R116" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461501">

1491116</a>

1492]. Similarly, endogenous cannabinoids have been shown to regulate airway responsiveness. In rodent lungs, a Ca<sup>

14932+</sup>

1494-activated mechanism for the biosynthesis of anandamide was observed and CB1 receptors were found predominantly on axon terminals of airway nerves, indicating that endocannabinoids may regulate bronchial smooth muscle tone [<a href="#R117" rid="R117" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461465">

1495117</a>

1496]. It was reported that activation of CB1 receptors by locally released anandamide may participate in the control of bronchial contractility. Blocking of AEA-induced CB1 activity can enhance capsaicin-induced bronchospasm. However, the authors further suggested that the effects of AEA may depend on the state of the bronchial muscle. During capsaicin-evoked bronchospasm, AEA may reduce the muscle contraction, whereas AEA may cause bronchoconstriction in the absence of vagus nerve-constricting tone [<a href="#R117" rid="R117" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461466">

1497117</a>

1498].</p>

1499<p id="P35">

1500Cannabidiol has been shown to be effective in protecting endothelial function and integrity in human coronary artery endothelial cells (HCAECs). The study demonstrated that CBD reversed the harmful effects of high glucose on HCAECs by inhibiting [<a href="#R118" rid="R118" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461406">

1501118</a>

1502]:</p>

1503<ul class="unordered" style="list-style-type:disc" id="L1">

1504<!--

1505list-behavior=unordered

1506prefix-word=

1507mark-type=disc

1508max-label-size=0

1509-->

1510<li>

1511<p id="P36">

1512Reactive oxygen species production by mitochondria</p>

1513</li>

1514<li>

1515<p id="P37">

1516NF-&#x003ba;B activation</p>

1517</li>

1518<li>

1519<p id="P38">

1520Transendothelial migration of monocytes</p>

1521</li>

1522<li>

1523<p id="P39">

1524Monocyte&#x02013;endothelial adhesion in HCAECs</p>

1525</li>

1526</ul>

1527<p id="P40">

1528In a different experiment, HCAECs were stimulated with TNF-&#x003b1; in order to mimic the inflammatory processes during atherosclerosis, and the effect of different CB2 receptor agonists on the activated cells was studied. It was demonstrated that activation of cells with TNF-&#x003b1; led to increased expression of CB2 and activation of Ras, p38, MAPK, JNK and AKT pathways. In addition, proliferation and migration was markedly increased in activated cell populations. The use of CB2 agonists JWH-133 and HU-308 inhibited all activated pathways in a dose-dependent manner, establishing a novel use for these cannabinoid compounds [<a href="#R119" rid="R119" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461493">

1529119</a>

1530].</p>

1531<p id="P41">

1532Experimental autoimmune uveoretinitis (EAU), is a CD4<sup>

1533+</sup>

1534 T-cell-mediated autoimmune disease, which can be induced in rodents by challenge with retinal antigens or their peptides [<a href="#R120" rid="R120" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461473">

1535120</a>

1536]. In a recent study, it was observed that the CB2-selective agonist JWH-133 had a high <em>

1537in vivo</em>

1538 immunosuppressive effect in EAU model. EAU was strongly inhibited when the CB2 was engaged and the effects of CB2 engagement appeared to be mediated predominantly through downregulation of T-cell function with a less-marked effect on antigen presentation [<a href="#R121" rid="R121" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461449">

1539121</a>

1540]. An impaired T-cell-proliferative response in leukocytes from JWH-133-treated mice was also accompanied by marked reductions in cytokine production. A more recent study showed that JWH-133 (10 nM&#x02013;5 &#x003bc;M) suppressed IL-12p40 and enhanced IL-10 production in mouse macrophages induced by LPS [<a href="#R122" rid="R122" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461460">

1541122</a>

1542]. As IL-12p40 is critical for the development of EAU and IL-10 could suppress EAU [<a href="#R123" rid="R123" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461495">

1543123</a>

1544,<a href="#R124" rid="R124" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461389">

1545124</a>

1546], the authors suggested that this may also be one of the possible mechanisms responsible for the effect of JWH-133 on EAU.</p>

1547<p id="P42" class="p p-last">

1548Insulin-dependent Type 1 diabetes mellitus (T1DM) is an autoimmune disease resulting in destruction of insulin-producing pancreatic &#x003b2; cells, a process that is assumed to be mediated mainly by CD4 Th1 and CD8 T lymphocytes [<a href="#R125" rid="R125" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461418">

1549125</a>

1550]. In rodents, T1D is induced by administration of multiple low doses of streptozotocin (MLDSTZ). This model is used for studying autoimmune processes associated with pancreatic &#x003b2;-cell pathogenesis. A study performed by Li <em>

1551et al.</em>

1552 indicated that &#x00394;<sup>

15539</sup>

1554-THC could exert a transient attenuation of MLDSTZ-induced autoimmune diabetes. &#x00394;<sup>

15559</sup>

1556-THC treated (150 mg/kg) CD-1 mice exhibited reduced hyperglycemia and a significant decrease in the loss of pancreatic insulin. MLDSTZ-induced insulitis was also significantly attenuated by decreases in CD3<sup>

1557+</sup>

1558 inflammatory cells in the pancreatic islets and in mRNA expression for IL-12, IFN-&#x003b3; and TNF-&#x003b1;. It was suggested that in this model, the autoimmune component was most effectively modulated by &#x00394;<sup>

15599</sup>

1560-THC treatment [<a href="#R126" rid="R126" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461401">

1561126</a>

1562]. Similarly, CBD treatment has been shown to significantly inhibit and delay destructive insulitis and inflammatory Th1-associated cytokine production in nonobese diabetes-prone (NOD) female mice. CBD-treated mice exhibited significant reduction of plasma levels of the proinflammatory cytokines IFN-&#x003b3; and TNF-&#x003b1;, whereas production of the Th2-associated cytokines IL-4 and IL-10 was increased when compared with untreated control mice, thus shifting the immune response from Th1 to Th2 dominance [<a href="#R127" rid="R127" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461446">

1563127</a>

1564]. A recent study indicated that treatment of 11&#x02013;14-week-old female NOD mice, either in a latent diabetes stage (after 14 weeks) or with initial symptoms of diabetes (appearing up to 14 weeks) with CBD for 4 weeks, could lead to sustained inhibition of insulitis [<a href="#R128" rid="R128" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461479">

1565128</a>

1566]. CBD treatment inhibited specific destruction of the islets and reduced the infiltrates by mononuclear cells into the islets, thus preventing diabetes. Furthermore, cannabinoids have also been demonstrated to possess additional beneficial effects in animal models of diabetes. It has been reported that rats treated with CBD for periods of 1&#x02013;4 weeks experienced significant protection from diabetic retinopathy [<a href="#R129" rid="R129" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461360">

1567129</a>

1568]. Cannabinoids have also been shown to alleviate neuropathic pain associated with the disease. Mice injected with a cannabis receptor agonist experienced a reduction in diabetic-related tactile allodynia compared with nontreated controls [<a href="#R130" rid="R130" class=" bibr popnode tag_hotlink tag_tooltip" id="__tag_276461475">

1569130</a>

1570]. Thus, cannabinoids can be considered useful for controlling T1D due to their anti-inflammatory properties.</p>

1571</div>

1572<div id="S9" class="tsec sec">

1573<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="S9title">

1574Future perspective</h2>

1575<p id="P43" class="p p-first">

1576It is becoming increasingly clear that cannabinoid receptors and their endogenous ligands play a crucial role in the regulation of the immune system. Exogenous cannabinoids have been shown to suppress T-cell-mediated immune responses by primarily inducing apoptosis and suppressing inflammatory cytokines and chemokines. Such observations indicate that targeting cannabinoid receptor&#x02013;ligand interactions may constitute a novel window of opportunity to treat inflammatory and autoimmune disorders. As CB2 receptors are primarily expressed on immune cells, targeting CB2 may result in selective immunomodulation without overt toxicity. The future challenges for the use of cannabinoids as anti-inflammatory drugs include synthesis of cannabinoid receptor agonists that are nonpsychoactive with anti-inflammatory activity and then identifying their mode of action. Although current studies suggest that cannabinoids are useful therapeutic agents in the treatment of various inflammatory disorders, further evaluation of the mechanisms that account for their anti-inflammatory properties is necessary. Such studies may involve the use of cannabinoid receptor-knockout mice and use of receptor-specific compounds. While most studies have focused on the effect of cannabinoids on cytokines, apoptosis and Th1 cell functions in the past, additional investigations on their effect on Th17 cells, DCs, NK cells, B cells and Fox-P3<sup>

1577+</sup>

1578 regulatory T cells is critical as such cells play an important role in the regulation and mediation of inflammatory or autoimmune disease response. Whether endocannabinoids and cannabinoid receptors play a critical role during normal inflammatory response also requires further consideration. Moreover, cannabinoid receptor signaling and effect of cannabinoids on adhesion molecules, co-stimulatory molecules and chemokines require further study in order to increase our understanding of cannabinoids and their intricate effects on immune system disorders. Overall, cannabinoids have exhibited significant potential to be used as novel anti-inflammatory agents and specific targeting of CB2 receptors holds the promise of mediating immunosuppressive effects without exerting psychotropic side effects.</p>

1579<div class="boxed-text-box whole_rhythm hide-overflow" id="BX1">

1580<div class="sec" id="S10">

1581<h3 id="S10title">

1582Executive summary</h3>

1583<ul class="unordered" style="list-style-type:disc" id="L2">

1584<!--

1585list-behavior=unordered

1586prefix-word=

1587mark-type=disc

1588max-label-size=0

1589-->

1590<li>

1591<p id="P44">

1592Cannabinoids, the active components of <em>

1593Cannabis sativa,</em>

1594 and endogenous cannabinoids mediate their effects through activation of specific cannabinoid receptors known as cannabinoid receptor 1 and 2 (CB1 and CB2).</p>

1595</li>

1596<li>

1597<p id="P45">

1598The cannabinoid system has been shown both <em>

1599in vivo</em>

1600 and <em>

1601in vitro</em>

1602 to be involved in regulating the immune system through its immunomodulatory properties.</p>

1603</li>

1604<li>

1605<p id="P46">

1606Cannabinoids suppress inflammatory response and subsequently attenuate disease symptoms. This property of cannabinoids is mediated through multiple pathways such as induction of apoptosis in activated immune cells, suppression of cytokines and chemokines at inflammatory sites and upregulation of FoxP3<sup>

1607+</sup>

1608 regulatory T cells.</p>

1609</li>

1610<li>

1611<p id="P47">

1612Cannabinoids have been tested in several experimental models of autoimmune disorders such as multiple sclerosis, rheumatoid arthritis, colitis and hepatitis and have been shown to protect the host from the pathogenesis through induction of multiple anti-inflammatory pathways.</p>

1613</li>

1614<li>

1615<p id="P48">

1616Cannabinoids may also be beneficial in certain types of cancers that are triggered by chronic inflammation. In such instances, cannabinoids can either directly inhibit tumor growth or suppress inflammation and tumor angiogenesis.</p>

1617</li>

1618</ul>

1619</div>

1620</div>

1621</div>

1630<div id="GL" class="tsec sec">

1631<h2 class="head no_bottom_margin" id="GLtitle">

1632Glossary</h2>

1633<div class="bk-sec">

1634<div class="def-list">

1635<dl class="labeled-list">

1636<dt id="G1">

1637Cannabinoids</dt>

1638<dd>

1639Active components extracted from <em>

1640Cannabis sativa</em>

1641 that act through cannabinoid receptors</dd>

1642<dt id="G2">

1643Cannabinoid receptors</dt>

1644<dd>

1645G-protein-coupled receptors that mediate the action of cannabinoids</dd>

1646<dt id="G3">

1647Cytokines</dt>

1648<dd>

1649Signaling proteins synthesized and secreted by immune cells upon activation</dd>

1650<dt id="G4">

1651Autoimmune diseases</dt>

1652<dd>

1653Disorders in which the immune system starts recognizing an individual&#x02019;s own cells as foreign and mounts a reaction, leading to tissue injury</dd>

1654<dt id="G5">

1655Inflammation</dt>

1656<dd>

1657A defense mechanism that results from the action of activated immune cells in response to foreign antigens. Sometimes, response to self antigens can trigger severe tissue injury</dd>

1658</dl>

4005 (function($){

4006 $('.skiplink').each(function(i, item){

4011 $.scrollTo(href, 0, {
